0380018179|3|This is my kind of book: an epic family saga with a strong sense of place. I hate to give it only three stars, but I've had to conclude that while it has its strengths, it doesn't live up to what it could have been.<br /><br />The Thorn Birds starts off excellently, detailing the day-to-day life of a family struggling to get by in early twentieth century New Zealand. It's well-written, it feels very realistic, and there's a strong sense of place. There's believable conflict among the family members without any of them being unsympathetic. There's an interesting and unflinching look at the effects of strict gender roles on average women's lives: something we don't see in most historical fiction, which tends to feature the elite rather than regular folks. (I'll read about the regular folks any day, and McCullough does it well.) When the family picks up and moves to Australia, I was still enthralled. I loved the descriptions of life in the Outback and was drawn into the family's story.<br /><br />Somewhere along the way though, things went wrong. The characters' personalities and relationships began to lose credibility with me; several times I just couldn't swallow that people in these situations would relate to each other the way they do. Meggie's relationships with both her mother and her daughter are especially bizarre, full of contrived antagonism far beyond what one would expect. (In the daughter's case, evidently she dislikes Meggie from birth. Ooookay.) Some of the more colorful personalities, such as Frank and Luke, disappear partway through, while Meggie's tribe of brothers lose what personality they once had and slowly merge into one person, as if McCullough changed her mind about how many brothers the story required but couldn't be bothered to get rid of the extras. As for the romance between Meggie and Ralph, while at first it raised some interesting questions, it never captured my emotions and becomes increasingly repetitive.<br /><br />Which is not to say that this is an awful book, because it isn't. It's well-written and the thematics are strong. The strong descriptions and the sense of place persist throughout, and it's fascinating to see how the coming of new technology affects the Outback. The main characters are decently well-developed, and while the book is long, the plot remains interesting. I finished it in a few days. Still, if you have not yet read the classic historical epics, like Isabel Allende's House of the Spirits or Margaret Mitchell's Gone With the Wind, I'd recommend going with those first.that this is an awful book, because it isn't. 0307346609|5|I've been a fan of the zombie film since Night of the Living Dead. The premise, to me, has always been one of the most intriguing and terrifying of any set forth in the horror/sci-fi canon. Sure, 1984 and others painted a chilling picture of a world gone horribly wrong, but the idea of an epidemic- an incurable, rapidly spreading apocalypse- has always struck me as particularly scary.<br /><br />This is not a satire- there is no tongue and cheek present, despite what one may gather from the title and it's subheading (World War Z: An Oral History of the Zombie War). Sure, there are references to present conflicts and heads of state, as well as familiar terminology from the present. But it's not a parody- it is an honest and bracing attempt to hypothesize how the current world at large- with machinations such as globalization, the internet, and communist-cum-capitalist superpowers, for example- would deal with a crisis on such a massive scale. The short answer is \\"not well\\". The long answer, involving everything from America's advanced (and now useless) weapon systems to China's secrecy to Apartheid South Africa's ruthless and purposeful efficiency- is much more complicated, and much more thought provoking.<br /><br />The basic premise of the work, betraying nowhere its label of \\"fiction\\", down to the pull quotes and jacket, is this: In the first half of the 21st century, amidst all the well-known perpetual conflicts and cultures of the day, disaster strikes. It starts with an incident that will be familiar to anyone who's read \\"The Hot Zone\\" or \\"Demon in the Freezer\\". A single patient- Patient Zero- who is a resident of a rural Chinese province. From there, it escalates and spreads across the globe, taking an agonizingly long time to be identified for what it is. In China, it's a \\"contained health issue\\". In Capetown, it's \\"African Rabies\\" (in a nod to the attibution of a disease such as AIDS to a certain culture or population). In America, it's a virus treatable bya miracle drug called \\"Phalanx\\", rushed through the FDA as a placebo and eagerly snatched up by the population. In Israel, one of the few places to recognize the nature of the epidemic and secure itself, it is another Holocaust, one that can be prevented by sealing its borders and fortifying itself.<br /><br />After the initial outbreaks comes the incident referred to in the book as \\"The Great Panic\\", when the nature of what is happening is clear to every man, woman and child on the planet, with predictable results.<br /><br />All of these incidents- and many, many more- are laid out in the form of interviews with the people involved. From military personnel to diplomats to refugees to chiefs of staff to doctors, multiple viewpoints are portrayed. And realistically. As an example, I'll go with the military aspect. The military interviewees, especially American soldiers, refer to their enemy as \\"Zack\\", or \\"Gs\\" (\\"ghouls\\"). They routinely chastise their superiors for employing outdated methods to combat the threat- after all, what good is stealth weaponry to an enemy with no radar? What's the point of infrared when \\"Zack\\" doesn't give off any heat? Why is emphasis placed on \\"cover\\" when the threat can't fire a weapon? There is no condescending portrayal of the military here- only of the bureaucracy that pulls the strings.<br /><br />At the risk of spoiling the whole experience for the reader, I'll stop here. There are many suprises and heatbreaking stories in this work (one of the greatest being the Plan developed to save human race, with terrifying consequences).<br /><br />If you're a genre fan, pick it up. If you have an interest in hypothetical history, pick it up. It's a great read, and suprisingly thought provoking.

B000Q3648S|5|The fifth album (more or less) by The Best Rock Band of the Last 25 Years should need no introduction, and indeed if you're interested in buying the deluxe edition of Sonic Youth's \\"Daydream Nation,\\" I can presume you're probably already a fan.  You already know how the album summed up and then changed the course of the underground rock movement of the 80s, how the ample attention of critics, then music fans, then record labels set the stage for the alternative rock revolution of the 90s--oh well whatever [[ASIN:B000003TA4 Nevermind]] and yada yada yada.  If, however, you haven't already drank the Kool-Aid (and you should!), some brief notes about the \\"basic\\" edition...<br /><br />\\"Daydream Nation\\" is still hailed as Sonic Youth's grand masterpiece, and yeah, it still holds up as such (confession: it's my second favorite SY album after [[ASIN:B000003TAJ Sister]]), even if it doesn't seem as radical today as it did when it came out.  Sure, there are swirling whirlwinds of oddly-(de)tuned guitars and feedback fading in, out and sometimes interrupting the songs, but make no mistake, there are definitely songs to be found here.  The openening \\"Teenage Riot\\" is, for its galloping drums and lyrical cheekiness (imagining Dinosaur Jr's J. Mascis as president, or so Thurston Moore has said) basically a melodic and even hummable pop song.  Even a more \\"experimental\\" song like \\"The Sprawl\\" has a certain immediacy, with bassist/vocalist Kim Gordon saying straigt out \\"does f**k you sound simple enough?\\"  Heck, the closing \\"trilogy\\" of the album has all the power and intensity familiar to any classic rock fans, only with a live-wire buzzing and skree that comes from playing in one of Glenn Branca's guitar-army ensembles.  Really, it's not so radical after all (for a taste of something far weirder, check out [[ASIN:B0000019PB Twin Infinitives]], which was released around the same time as DN by Royal Trux, a New York band friendly with Sonic Youth).<br /><br />For us fan(atic)s, the real treat here is the second disc of this set, which features live versions of all the album's tracks, culled from the band's original tour supporting the album, albeit in a different order (SY has recently announced it will play \\"Daydream Nation\\" as part of a special show, presumably in the album's running order).  These tracks, mostly of excellent sound quality, are shorter but sharper than the album versions.  \\"Total Trash\\" is done as an instrumental (I could be wrong, but they seem to be quoting the early Pink Floyd's instrumental \\"Interstellar Overdrive\\" in one spot), preceded by another instrumental, \\"Totally Trashed,\\" which toys around with electronics a bit.  Mostly these are no-holds-barred performances that clearly draw on the band's love of hardcore punk and NYC No Wave (DNA, Teenage Jesus & the Jerks).  I don't know how a non-fan would react, but for yours truly it's pure Sonic bliss.<br /><br />Another great bonus on disc two is the inclusion of four cover songs, three of which were taken from now hard-to-find tribute albums (this was before that concept got beaten to death): \\"Within You Without You\\" (The Beatles), \\"Computer Age\\" (Neil Young) and \\"Electricity\\" (Captain Beefheart).  There's also a version of Mudhoney's \\"Touch Me I'm Sick,\\" with demented vocals from Kim, taken from a split 7\\" (as in vinyl, kids) that had the other band doing SY's \\"Halloween\\" on the reverse (alas, that version isn't included here).  The Beatles cover in particular shows how the band could translate their sound into completely different realms--the original, of course, was played mostly with sitars.<br /><br />Add all that to a package that includes ultra-Kool, and rare, pictures and an extensive essay by longtime SY friend Byron Coley, and you have a true Sonic feast.  Like they did with the [[ASIN:B000BR2P82 Goo Deluxe Edition]] and the [[ASIN:B000083LQB Dirty (Deluxe Edition)]], Sonic Youth rewards fans and newcomers alike almost to the bursting point.  Now let's hopethey burn down the Rock n' Roll Hall of Fame before too long...Hall of Fame before too long...

B005HEVBGG|4|When Voyager first hit the airways I was not impressed.  TO be honest, Janeway was the reason why. But I was so head-in-clouds over Deep Space Nine and Babylon 5 that I acknowledged not really giving the show a fair chance.  So I decided to watch it from the beginning.  And I will write high level reviews for each season, not each episode.<br /><br />Season 1's focus is the crew getting adjusted to life in the Delta Quadrant as well as the merging of Maquis and Starfleet members into a cohesive crew.  This struggle makes for some good writing early on as you would definitely expect some conflicts, but there were some missed opportunities for sabotage.  Episodes like \\"State of Flux\\" which sets the foundation for one of the most interesting backstories in Voyager (Seska) and \\"Learning Curve\\" where they showcase some stragglers that just didn't make the full transition to Starfleet life.  The most obvious negative is that they never fully explored the latter; after a while, people just start flowing along happily, which given the story of the Maquis told in Deep Space Nine, should really been more challenging.<br /><br />There were some head scratcher episodes, such as \\"Time and Again\\" where it causes a headache thinking about what's going on (which is the reason it's generally not a good idea to tell a time travel-related story), and you can't help but think back to TNG's \\"Time's Arrow\\" where Data experiences generally the same idea; that you can encounter an event today that both hasn't and has happened.  Trust me when I say that the episode does make sense, but it will give you some migraines trying to sort through the chronology of everything.  Even worse is the idea that Janeway can affect the flow of time by realizing the paradox, thus preventing the event that caused them to be in the situation in the first place (thus creating another paradox).<br /><br />Other episodes,like \\"Emanations\\" are filler and do little to further the series.  Annoyances that are better left out of the canon, but we're stuck with nonetheless.  \\"Prime Factors\\" is actually a well-told story marred by poor character interactions and illogical behavior (Tuvok).<br /><br />It's clear from the episode progression that the writers were trying to use this opportunity to work with characters and storylines that they could not have in the other Star Trek versions.  Being in the Delta Quadrant meant they could make up characters at a whim, and throwing the Maquis in there gave them \\"bad guys\\" without much effort.  The problem is that the approach is so haphazard that it will be difficult to really be interested in seeing more seasons, but the first season is necessary to really enjoy some of what comes later.

B00BPDR3F6|4|Henry Whitaker was probably one of the first pharmaceutical companies.  He found a treatment for malaria and made tons of money selling this bark derivative to the masses.  Alma is his only biological child.  What a bright and beautiful mind she has--constantly questioning, striving to learn and understand.  She also possesses the ability to observe all things in nature.  However Alma is not packaged in a petite attractive feminine body.  Does this packaging negate her dreams of actual physical love preclude her from achieving these as part of her reality?<br /><br />Although observant, Alma is unable to read people clearly.  This results in misunderstandings and tragic lives.  What does Alma gain by the end of her life that is worth the pain of a life lived?  Will her love of botany and science be able to understand, process and balance the spiritual and mystic?<br /><br />Honestly after posting about my vehement hatred of Elizabeth's Gilbert's &#34;Eat, Pray, Love&#34; I felt like I needed to give one of her novels a chance.  I am glad I did.<br /><br />This is a captivating novel, although the botanical discussions can be a bit tedious, with insights into life of this realm and life of the realms we do not know about...

0345533666|5|Within just a few pages of reading Defending Jacob, I was hooked.  Author William Landy hits a perfectly mystifying tone.<br /><br />Lead assistant district attorney Andrew Barber's adolescent son, Jacob, is accussed of murdering a classmate.  In the beginning hours of the murder investigation, Mr. Barber is in charge of handling the DA's case.  When it becomes apparent that his son is the main suspect, another assistant attorney, Neal Logiudice takes over.<br /><br />This novel is peppered with transcripts of grand jury testimony where tense comments are traded between Mr. Logiudice and the 'witness', Andrew Barber.  This adds to building questions and suspense that surround Defending Jacob.<br /><br />Andy holds on tight to the belief that his son did not comitt murder.  Despite strange revelations about his son and the strain on his wife, he still holds on to the belief that his son is innocent.  It is inconceivable to him that a father could think otherwise.<br /><br />As a former district attorney, author, William Landy brings a realistic inside look into the criminal system.  I had read that this novel had a surprising conclusion.  I was trying to guess what it might be while reading.  In the end, I was surprised.<br /><br />First Paragraph<br /><br />1   In the Grand Jury<br /><br />Mr. Logiudice: State your name, please.<br /><br />Witness: Andrew Barber.<br /><br />Mr. Logiudice: What do you do for work, Mr. Barber?<br /><br />Witness: I was an assistant district attorney in this county for 22 years.<br /><br />Mr. Logiudice: &#34;Was.&#34; What do you do for work now?<br /><br />Witness: I suppose you'd say I'm unemployed.<br /><br />In April 2008, Neal Logiudice finally subpoenaed me to appear before the grand jury.  By then it was too late.  Too late for his case, certainly, but also too late for Logiudice.  His reputation was already damaged beyond repair, and his career along with it.  A prosecutor can limp along with a damaged reputation for awhile, but his colleagues will watch him like wolves and eventually he will be forced out, for the good of the pack.  I have seen it many times: an ADA is irreplaceable one day, forgotten the next.&#34;<br /><br />About The Author (from the inside jacket cover)<br /><br />&#34;WILLIAM LANDAY is the author of The Strangler, a Los Angeles Times Favorite Crime Book of the Year, and Mission Flats, winner of the Creasey Memorial Dagger Award for Best First Crime Novel and a Barry Award nominee.
